<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Environmental impact of aviation</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:C-141_Starlifter_contrail_crop1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1900" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/C-141_Starlifter_contrail_crop1.png/250px-C-141_Starlifter_contrail_crop1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/C-141_Starlifter_contrail_crop1.png/375px-C-141_Starlifter_contrail_crop1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/C-141_Starlifter_contrail_crop1.png/500px-C-141_Starlifter_contrail_crop1.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:C-141_Starlifter_contrail_crop1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C-141_Starlifter" title="C-141 Starlifter">C-141 Starlifter</a> leaves <a href="/wiki/Contrail" title="Contrail">contrails</a> over <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>environmental impact of aviation</b> occurs because <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_engine" title="Aircraft engine">aircraft engines</a> emit heat, <a href="/wiki/Noise_pollution" title="Noise pollution">noise</a>, particulates, and gases which contribute to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICAO_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICAO-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enviro.aeroImpact_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Enviro.aeroImpact-2">[2]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Global_dimming" title="Global dimming">global dimming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-contrails_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-contrails-3">[3]</a></sup> Airplanes emit particles and gases such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>), water vapor, <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_oxide" title="Sulfur oxide">sulfur oxides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Black_carbon" title="Black carbon">black carbon</a> which interact among themselves and with the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite emission reductions from automobiles and more fuel-efficient and less polluting <a href="/wiki/Turbofan" title="Turbofan">turbofan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turboprop" title="Turboprop">turboprop</a> engines, the rapid growth of <a href="/wiki/Air_travel" title="Air travel">air travel</a> in the past years contributes to an increase in total pollution attributable to <a href="/wiki/Aviation" title="Aviation">aviation</a>. From 1992 to 2005, passenger kilometers increased 5.2% per year. And in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions from aviation increased by 87% between 1990 and 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Comprehensive research shows that despite anticipated efficiency innovations to airframes, engines, aerodynamics and flight operations, there is no end in sight, even many decades out, to rapid growth in CO<sub>2</sub> emissions from air travel and air freight,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> due to projected continual growth in air travel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> This is because international aviation emissions have escaped international regulation up to the ICAO triennial conference in October 2016 agreed on the CORSIA offset scheme,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and because of the lack of <a href="/wiki/Aviation_fuel#Tax" title="Aviation fuel">taxes on aviation fuel</a> worldwide, lower fares become more frequent than otherwise, which gives a competitive advantage over other transportation modes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Unless market constraints are put in place, this growth in aviation's emissions will result in the sector's emissions amounting to all or nearly all of the annual global <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> emissions budget by mid-century, if climate change is to be held to a temperature increase of 2 °C or less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is an ongoing debate about possible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxation" title="Taxation">taxation</a> of air travel and the inclusion of aviation in an <a href="/wiki/Emissions_trading" title="Emissions trading">emissions trading</a> scheme, with a view to ensuring that the total <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/External_costs" title="External costs">external costs</a> of aviation are taken into account.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radiative_Forcing_in_Aviation_1992.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2023" data-file-width="3329" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Radiative_Forcing_in_Aviation_1992.png/350px-Radiative_Forcing_in_Aviation_1992.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Radiative_Forcing_in_Aviation_1992.png/525px-Radiative_Forcing_in_Aviation_1992.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Radiative_Forcing_in_Aviation_1992.png/700px-Radiative_Forcing_in_Aviation_1992.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radiative_Forcing_in_Aviation_1992.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Radiative forcings from aviation emissions (gases and aerosols) in 1992 as estimated by the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">IPCC</a></div></div></div>
<p>Like all human activities involving <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a>, most forms of <a href="/wiki/Aviation" title="Aviation">aviation</a> release <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>) and other greenhouse gases into the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, contributing to the acceleration of <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-anderson-12">[12]</a></sup> and (in the case of CO<sub>2</sub>) <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> These concerns are highlighted by the present volume of commercial aviation and its rate of growth. Globally, about 8.3 million people fly daily (3 billion occupied seats per year), twice the total in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> U.S. airlines alone burned about 16.2 billion gallons of fuel during the twelve months between October 2013 and September 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to the CO<sub>2</sub> released by most aircraft in flight through the burning of fuels such as <a href="/wiki/Jet_fuel" title="Jet fuel">Jet-A</a> (turbine aircraft) or <a href="/wiki/Avgas" title="Avgas">Avgas</a> (piston aircraft), the aviation industry also contributes <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions from ground <a href="/wiki/Airport" title="Airport">airport</a> vehicles and those used by passengers and staff to access airports, as well as through emissions generated by the production of energy used in airport buildings, the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">aircraft</a> and the construction of airport infrastructure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the principal <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emission from powered aircraft in flight is CO<sub>2</sub>, other emissions may include <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">nitric oxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_dioxide" title="Nitrogen dioxide">nitrogen dioxide</a> (together termed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxides_of_nitrogen" title="Oxides of nitrogen">oxides of nitrogen</a> or NO<sub>x</sub>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_vapour" title="Water vapour">water vapour</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_particulate_matter" title="Atmospheric particulate matter">particulates</a> (soot and sulfate particles), sulfur oxides, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (which bonds with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> to become CO<sub>2</sub> immediately upon release), incompletely burned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocarbons" title="Hydrocarbons">hydrocarbons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tetraethyllead" title="Tetraethyllead">tetraethyllead</a> (piston aircraft only), and radicals such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a>, depending on the type of aircraft in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-17">[17]</a></sup> Emissions weighting factor (EWFs) i.e., the factor by which aviation CO<sub>2</sub> emissions should be multiplied to get the CO<sub>2</sub>-equivalent emissions for annual fleet average conditions is in the range 1.3–2.9.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanisms_and_cumulative_effects_of_aviation_on_climate">Mechanisms and cumulative effects of aviation on climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mechanisms and cumulative effects of aviation on climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1999 the contribution of civil aircraft-in-flight to global CO<sub>2</sub> emissions was estimated to be around 2%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-17">[17]</a></sup> However, in the cases of high-altitude <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airliners" title="Airliners">airliners</a> which frequently fly near or in the <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>, non-CO<sub>2</sub> altitude-sensitive effects may increase the total impact on anthropogenic (human-made) <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> significantly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-17">[17]</a></sup> A 2007 report from <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Change_Institute" title="Environmental Change Institute">Environmental Change Institute</a> / Oxford University posits a range closer to 4 percent cumulative effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
Subsonic aircraft-in-flight contribute to climate change<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-17">[17]</a></sup> in four ways:
</p>
<h4><span id="Carbon_dioxide_.28CO2.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_dioxide_(CO2)">Carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Carbon dioxide (CO2)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>CO<sub>2</sub> emissions from aircraft-in-flight are the most significant and best understood<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sausen_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sausen-20">[20]</a></sup> element of aviation's total contribution to climate change. The level and effects of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions are currently believed to be broadly the same regardless of altitude (i.e. they have the same atmospheric effects as ground-based emissions). In 1992, emissions of CO<sub>2</sub> from aircraft were estimated at around 2% of all such anthropogenic emissions, and that year the atmospheric concentration of CO<sub>2</sub> attributable to aviation was around 1% of the total anthropogenic increase since the industrial revolution, having accumulated primarily over just the last 50 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_chapter4_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_chapter4-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Oxides_of_nitrogen_.28NOx.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides_of_nitrogen_(NOx)">Oxides of nitrogen (NO<sub>x</sub>)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Oxides of nitrogen (NOx)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>At the high altitudes flown by large jet airliners around the <a href="/wiki/Tropopause" title="Tropopause">tropopause</a>, emissions of NO<sub>x</sub> are particularly effective in forming <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> (O<sub>3</sub>) in the upper <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a>. High altitude (8–13 km) NO<sub>x</sub> emissions result in greater concentrations of O<sub>3</sub> than surface NO<sub>x</sub> emissions, and these in turn have a greater global warming effect. The effect of O<sub>3</sub> concentrations are regional and local (as opposed to CO<sub>2</sub> emissions, which are global).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>NO<sub>x</sub> emissions also reduce ambient levels of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, another greenhouse gas, resulting in a climate cooling effect. But this effect does not offset the O<sub>3</sub> forming effect of NO<sub>x</sub> emissions. It is now believed that aircraft <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> and water emissions in the <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a> tend to deplete O<sub>3</sub>, partially offsetting the NO<sub>x</sub>-induced O<sub>3</sub> increases. These effects have not been quantified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_chapter4_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_chapter4-21">[21]</a></sup>    This problem does not apply to aircraft that fly lower in the troposphere, such as light aircraft or many commuter aircraft.
</p>
<h4><span id="Water_vapor_.28H2O.29.2C_and_contrails"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_vapor_(H2O),_and_contrails">Water vapor (H<sub>2</sub>O), and contrails</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Water vapor (H2O), and contrails">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Contrails.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="263" data-file-width="400" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Contrails.jpg/220px-Contrails.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Contrails.jpg/330px-Contrails.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/85/Contrails.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Contrails.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Condensation_trails" title="Condensation trails">Contrails</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cirrus_clouds_cold_day.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1924" data-file-width="2574" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Cirrus_clouds_cold_day.jpg/220px-Cirrus_clouds_cold_day.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Cirrus_clouds_cold_day.jpg/330px-Cirrus_clouds_cold_day.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Cirrus_clouds_cold_day.jpg/440px-Cirrus_clouds_cold_day.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cirrus_clouds_cold_day.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cirrus_cloud" title="Cirrus cloud">Cirrus cloud</a> formation</div></div></div>
<p>One of the products of burning hydrocarbons with oxygen is water vapour, a greenhouse gas. Water vapour produced by aircraft engines at high altitude, under certain atmospheric conditions, condenses into droplets to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Condensation_trails" title="Condensation trails">Condensation trails</a>, or contrails. Contrails are visible line clouds that form in cold, humid atmospheres and are thought to have a global warming effect (though one less significant than either CO<sub>2</sub> emissions or NO<sub>x</sub> induced effects).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Contrails are uncommon (though by no means rare) from lower-altitude aircraft, or from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propeller_(aircraft)" title="Propeller (aircraft)">propeller</a>-driven aircraft or rotorcraft.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cirrus_cloud" title="Cirrus cloud">Cirrus clouds</a> have been observed to develop after the persistent formation of contrails and have been found to have a global warming effect over-and-above that of contrail formation alone. There is a degree of scientific uncertainty about the contribution of contrail and cirrus cloud formation to global warming and attempts to estimate aviation's overall climate change contribution do not tend to include its effects on cirrus cloud enhancement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sausen_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sausen-20">[20]</a></sup> However, a 2015 study found that artificial cloudiness caused by contrail "outbreaks" reduces the difference between daytime and nighttime temperatures. The former are decreased and the latter are increased, in comparison to temperatures the day before and the day after such outbreaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> On days with outbreaks the day/night temperature difference was diminished by about 6F° in the U.S. South and 5F° in the Midwest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Particulates">Particulates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Particulates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Least significant on a mass basis is the release of soot and sulfate particles. Soot absorbs heat and has a warming effect; sulfate particles reflect radiation and have a small cooling effect. In addition, particles can influence the formation and properties of clouds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> including both line-shaped contrails and naturally-occurring cirrus clouds. The impact of "spreading contrails and cirrus clouds that evolve from them -- collectively known as contrail cirrus -- have a greater radiative forcing (RF) today than all aviation CO<sub>2</sub> emissions since the first powered airplane flight".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Of the particles emitted by aircraft engines, the soot particles are thought to be most important for contrail formation since they are large enough to serve as condensation nuclei for water vapor. All aircraft powered by combustion will release some amount of soot; although, recent studies suggest that reducing the aromatic content of jet fuel decreases the amount of soot produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenhouse_gas_emissions_per_passenger_kilometre">Greenhouse gas emissions per passenger kilometre</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Greenhouse gas emissions per passenger kilometre">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Averaged_emissions">Averaged emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Averaged emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Emissions of passenger aircraft per passenger kilometre vary extensively because of differing factors such as the size and type aircraft, the altitude and the percentage of passenger or freight capacity of a particular flight, and the distance of the journey and number of stops en route. Also, the effect of a given amount of emissions on climate (<a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a>) is greater at higher altitudes: <a href="#Total_climate_effects">see below</a>. Some representative figures for CO<sub>2</sub> emissions are provided by LIPASTO's survey of average direct emissions (not accounting for high-altitude radiative effects) of airliners expressed as CO<sub>2</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub> equivalent per passenger kilometre:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Domestic, short distance, less than 463 km (288 mi): 257 g/km CO<sub>2</sub> or 259 g/km (14.7 oz/mile) CO<sub>2</sub>e</li>
<li>Domestic, long distance, greater than 463 km (288 mi): 177 g/km CO<sub>2</sub> or 178 g/km (10.1 oz/mile) CO<sub>2</sub>e</li>
<li>Long distance flights: 113 g/km CO<sub>2</sub> or 114 g/km (6.5 oz/mile) CO<sub>2</sub>e</li></ul><p>
These emissions are similar to a four-seat car with one person on board;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> however, flying trips often cover longer distances than would be undertaken by car, so the total emissions are much higher. For perspective, per passenger a typical economy-class New York to Los Angeles round trip produces about 715 kg (1574 lb) of CO<sub>2</sub> (but is equivalent to 1,917 kg (4,230 lb) of CO<sub>2</sub> when the high altitude "climatic forcing" effect is taken into account).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>  Within the categories of flights above, emissions from scheduled jet flights are substantially higher than turboprop or chartered jet flights. About 60% of aviation emissions arise from international flights, and these flights are not covered by the Kyoto Protocol and its emissions reduction targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Owen-33">[33]</a></sup> However, in a more recent development:</p><blockquote><p>The United Nations’ aviation arm overwhelmingly ratified an agreement Thursday (06.Oct.2016) to control global warming emissions from international airline flights, the first climate-change pact to set worldwide limits on a single industry. The agreement, adopted overwhelmingly by the 191-nation International Civil Aviation Organization at a meeting in Montreal, sets airlines’ carbon emissions in the year 2020 as the upper limit of what carriers are allowed to discharge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Figures from British Airways suggest carbon dioxide emissions of 100g per passenger kilometre for large jet airliners (a figure which does not account for the production of other pollutants or condensation trails).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><h4><span id="Emissions_by_passenger_class.2C_and_effects_of_seating_configuration"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Emissions_by_passenger_class,_and_effects_of_seating_configuration">Emissions by passenger class, and effects of seating configuration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Emissions by passenger class, and effects of seating configuration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2013 the World Bank published a study of the effect on CO<sub>2</sub> emissions of its staff's travel in business class or first class, versus using economy class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WB-2013_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-WB-2013-36">[36]</a></sup> Among the factors considered was that these premium classes displace proportionately more economy seats for the same total aircraft space capacity, and the associated differing load factors and weight factors. This was not accounted for in prior standard carbon accounting methods. The study concluded that when considering respective average load factors (percent of occupied seats) in each of the seating classes, the carbon footprints of business class and first class are three-times and nine-times higher than economy class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WB-2013_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-WB-2013-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICCT-2013_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICCT-2013-38">[38]</a></sup> A related article by the International Council on Clean Transport notes further regarding the effect of seating configurations on carbon emissions that:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICCT-2013_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-ICCT-2013-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The A380 is marketed as a "green giant" and one of the most environmentally advanced aircraft out there. But that spin is based on a maximum-capacity aircraft configuration, or about 850 economy passengers. In reality, a typical A380 aircraft has 525 seats. Its fuel performance is comparable to that of a B747-400 ER and even about 15% worse than a B777-300ER on a passenger-mile basis (calculated using Piano-5 on a flight from AUH to LHR, assuming an 80% passenger load factor, and in-service fleet average seat counts).
</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Total_climate_effects">Total climate effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Total climate effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In attempting to aggregate and quantify the total climate impact of aircraft emissions the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) has estimated that aviation's total climate impact is some 2–4 times that of its direct CO<sub>2</sub> emissions alone (excluding the potential impact of cirrus cloud enhancement).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-17">[17]</a></sup>  This is measured as <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a>. While there is uncertainty about the exact level of impact of NO<sub>x</sub> and water vapour, governments have accepted the broad scientific view that they do have an effect. Globally in 2005, aviation contributed "possibly as much as 4.9% of radiative forcing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Owen-33">[33]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_transport_and_the_environment_(United_Kingdom)#Airport_development_strategy" title="Air transport and the environment (United Kingdom)">UK government policy</a> statements have stressed the need for aviation to address its total climate change impacts and not simply the impact of CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The IPCC has estimated that aviation is responsible for around 3.5% of anthropogenic climate change, a figure which includes both CO<sub>2</sub> and non-CO<sub>2</sub> induced effects. The IPCC has produced scenarios estimating what this figure could be in 2050. The central case estimate is that aviation's contribution could grow to 5% of the total contribution by 2050 if action is not taken to tackle these emissions, though the highest scenario is 15%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-17">[17]</a></sup> Moreover, if other industries achieve significant cuts in their own greenhouse gas emissions, aviation's share as a proportion of the remaining emissions could also rise.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_emission_levels">Future emission levels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Future emission levels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Even though there have been significant improvements in <a href="/wiki/Fuel_efficiency" title="Fuel efficiency">fuel efficiency</a> through aircraft technology and operational management as described here, these improvements are being continually eclipsed by the increase in air traffic volume.
</p><p>A December 2015 report finds that aircraft could generate <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7013430000000000000♠</span>43 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">Gt</a></span> of carbon pollution through to 2050, consuming almost 5% of the remaining global climate budget.  Without regulation, global aviation emissions may triple by mid-century and could emit more than <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7012300000000000000♠</span>3 Gt</span> of carbon annually under a high-growth, business-as-usual scenario.  Efforts to bring aviation emissions under an effective global accord have so far largely failed, despite there being a number of technological and operational improvements on offer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-center-for-biological-diversity-2015_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-center-for-biological-diversity-2015-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paradee-2015_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-paradee-2015-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Continual_increases_in_travel_and_freight">Continual increases in travel and freight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Continual increases in travel and freight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>From 1992 to 2005, passenger kilometers increased 5.2% per year, even with the disruptions of 9/11 and two significant wars. Since the onset of the current recession:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>During the first three quarters of 2010, air travel markets expanded at an annualized rate approaching 10%. This is similar to the rate seen in the rapid expansion prior to the recession. November's results mean the annualized rate of growth so far in Q4 drops back to around 6%. But this is still in line with long run rates of traffic growth seen historically. The level of international air travel is now 4% above the pre-recession peak of early 2008 and the current expansion looks to have further to run.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IATANov2010_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-IATANov2010-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Air freight reached a new high point in May (2010) but, following the end of inventory restocking activity, volumes have slipped back to settle at a similar level seen just before the onset of recession. Even so, that means an expansion of air freight during 2010 of 5–6% on an annualized basis – close to historical trend. With the stimulus of inventory restocking activity removed, further growth in air freight demand will be driven by end consumer demand for goods which utilize the air transport supply chain. ... The end of the inventory cycle does not mean the end of volume expansion but markets are entering a slower growth phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IATANov2010_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-IATANov2010-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>In a 2008 presentation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-anderson-12">[12]</a></sup> and paper <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Professor <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Anderson_(scientist)" title="Kevin Anderson (scientist)">Kevin Anderson</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Tyndall_Centre" title="Tyndall Centre">Tyndall Centre for Climate Change Research</a> showed how continued aviation growth in the UK threatens the ability of that nation to meet CO<sub>2</sub> emission reduction goals necessary to contain the century-end temperature increase to even 4 or 6C°. (See also: the <a href="/wiki/4_Degrees_and_Beyond_International_Climate_Conference" title="4 Degrees and Beyond International Climate Conference"> 4 Degrees and Beyond International Climate Conference (2009)</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> and its proceedings.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> His charts show the projected domestic aviation carbon emission increase for the UK as growing from 11 MT in 2006 to 17 MT in 2012, at the UK's historic annual emission growth rate of 7%. Beyond 2012 if the growth rate were reduced to 3% yearly, carbon emissions in 2030 would be 28 MT, which is 70% of the UK's entire carbon emissions budget that year for all sectors of society. This work also suggests the foreseeable future which confronts many other nations that have high dependency on aviation. "Hypermobile Travelers",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> an academic study by <a href="/wiki/Stefan_G%C3%B6ssling" title="Stefan Gössling">Stefan Gössling</a> et al. (2009) in the book "Climate Change and Aviation",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> also points to the dilemma caused by the increasing <a href="/wiki/Hypermobility_(travel)" title="Hypermobility (travel)">hypermobility of air travelers</a> both in particular nations and globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scope_for_improvement">Scope for improvement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Scope for improvement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aircraft_efficiency">Aircraft efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Aircraft efficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>While it is true that late model jet aircraft are significantly more fuel efficient (and thus emit less CO<sub>2</sub> in particular) than the earliest jet airliners,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peeters-2005_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peeters-2005-50">[50]</a></sup> new airliner models in the 2000s were barely more efficient on a seat-mile basis than the latest piston-powered airliners of the late 1950s (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lockheed_Starliner" title="Lockheed Starliner">Constellation L-1649-A</a> and <a href="/wiki/Douglas_DC-7" title="Douglas DC-7">DC-7C</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peeters-2005_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peeters-2005-50">[50]</a></sup> Claims for a high gain in efficiency for airliners over recent decades (while true in part) has been biased high in most studies, by using the early inefficient models of jet airliners as a baseline. Those aircraft were optimized for increased revenue, including increased speed and cruising altitude, and were quite fuel inefficient in comparison to their piston-powered forerunners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peeters-2005_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peeters-2005-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, turboprop aircraft – probably in part because of their lower cruising speeds and altitudes (similar to the earlier piston-powered airliners) compared to jet airliners – play an obvious role in the overall fuel efficiency of major airlines that have regional carrier subsidiaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICCT-Kwan_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICCT-Kwan-51">[51]</a></sup> For example, although <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Airlines" title="Alaska Airlines">Alaska Airlines</a> scored at the top of a 2011–2012 fuel efficiency ranking, if its large regional carrier – turbo-prop equipped <a href="/wiki/Horizon_Air" title="Horizon Air">Horizon Air</a> – were dropped from the lumped-in consideration, the airline's ranking would be somewhat lower, as noted in the ranking study.
</p><p>Aircraft manufacturers are striving for reductions in both CO<sub>2</sub> and NOx emissions with each new generation of design of aircraft and engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> While the introduction of more modern aircraft represents an opportunity to reduce emissions per passenger kilometre flown, aircraft are major investments that endure for many decades, and replacement of the international fleet is therefore a long-term proposition which will greatly delay realizing the climate benefits of many kinds of improvements. Engines can be changed at some point, but nevertheless airframes have a long life.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Moreover, rather than being linear from one year to the next the improvements to efficiency tend to diminish over time, as reflected in the histories of both piston and jet powered aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peeters-2005_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-Peeters-2005-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2014 <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_assessment" title="Life-cycle assessment">life-cycle assessment</a> of the cradle-to-grave reduction in CO<sub>2</sub> by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon-fiber-reinforced_polymer" title="Carbon-fiber-reinforced polymer">carbon-fiber-reinforced polymer</a> (CFRP) airliner such as a Boeing 787 – including its manufacture, operations and eventual disposal – has shown that by 2050 such aircraft could reduce the airline industry's CO<sub>2</sub> emissions by 14–15%, compared use of conventional airliners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Timmis_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Timmis-53">[53]</a></sup> The benefit of CFRP technology is not higher than that amount of reduction, despite the lighter weight and substantially lower fuel consumption of such aircraft, "because of the limited fleet penetration by 2050 and the increased demand for air travel due to lower operating costs." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Timmis_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Timmis-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Operations_efficiency">Operations efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Operations efficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Research projects such as Boeing's <a href="/wiki/EcoDemonstrator" title="EcoDemonstrator">ecoDemonstrator</a> program have sought to identify ways of improving the efficiency of commercial aircraft operations. The U.S. government has encouraged such research through grant programs, including the FAA's Continuous Lower Energy, Emissions and Noise (CLEEN) program, and NASA's Environmentally Responsible Aviation (ERA) Project.
</p><p>Adding an electric drive to the airplane's nose wheel may improve fuel efficiency during ground handling. This addition would allow taxiing without the use of the main engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another proposed change is the integrating of an <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_Aircraft_Launch_System" title="Electromagnetic Aircraft Launch System">Electromagnetic Aircraft Launch System</a> to the airstrips of airports. Some companies such as Airbus are currently researching this possibility. The adding of EMALS would allow the civilian aircraft to use considerably less fuel (as a lot of fuel is used during take off, in comparison to cruising, when calculated per km flown). The idea is to have the aircraft take off at regular aircraft speed, and only use the catapult for take-off, not for landing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other opportunities arise from the optimization of airline timetables, route networks and flight frequencies to increase load factors (minimize the number of empty seats flown),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>  together with the optimization of airspace. However, these are each one-time gains, and as these opportunities are successively fulfilled, diminishing returns can be expected from the remaining opportunities.
</p><p>Another possible reduction of the climate-change impact is the limitation of cruise altitude of aircraft. This would lead to a significant reduction in high-altitude contrails for a marginal trade-off of increased flight time and an estimated 4% increase in CO<sub>2</sub> emissions. Drawbacks of this solution include very limited airspace capacity to do this, especially in Europe and North America and increased fuel burn because jet aircraft are less efficient at lower cruise altitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>While they are not suitable for long-haul or transoceanic flights, turboprop aircraft used for commuter flights bring two significant benefits: they often burn considerably less fuel per passenger mile, and they typically fly at lower altitudes, well inside the tropopause, where there are no concerns about ozone or contrail production.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_fuels">Alternative fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Alternative fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Aviation_fuel#Chemical_composition" title="Aviation fuel">Aviation_fuel § Chemical_composition</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aviation_fuel#LNG" title="Aviation fuel">Aviation_fuel § LNG</a></div>
<p>Some scientists and companies such as <a href="/wiki/GE_Aviation" title="GE Aviation">GE Aviation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_Fuels" title="Virgin Fuels">Virgin Fuels</a> are researching <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a> technology for use in jet aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBC24Feb08_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-CBC24Feb08-61">[61]</a></sup> Some aircraft engines, like the <a href="/wiki/Wilksch_WAM_series" title="Wilksch WAM series">Wilksch WAM120</a> can (being a 2-stroke Diesel engine) run on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Straight_vegetable_oil" title="Straight vegetable oil">straight vegetable oil</a>. Also, a number of <a href="/wiki/Lycoming_Engines" title="Lycoming Engines">Lycoming engines</a> <a href="/wiki/Aviation_fuel#Chemical_composition" title="Aviation fuel">run well on ethanol</a>.
</p><p>In addition, there are also several tests done combining regular petrofuels with a biofuel. For example, as part of this test <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_Atlantic_Airways" title="Virgin Atlantic Airways">Virgin Atlantic Airways</a> flew a <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747" title="Boeing 747">Boeing 747</a> from London <a href="/wiki/Heathrow_Airport" title="Heathrow Airport">Heathrow Airport</a> to Amsterdam <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schiphol_Airport" title="Schiphol Airport">Schiphol Airport</a> on 24 February 2008, with one engine burning a combination of <a href="/wiki/Coconut_oil" title="Coconut oil">coconut oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Attalea_speciosa" title="Attalea speciosa">babassu oil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBC24Feb08_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-CBC24Feb08-61">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a>'s chief scientist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Doug_Parr&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Doug Parr (page does not exist)">Doug Parr</a> said that the flight was "high-altitude <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenwash" title="Greenwash">greenwash</a>" and that producing organic oils to make <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a> could lead to deforestation and a large increase in greenhouse gas emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBC24Feb08_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-CBC24Feb08-61">[61]</a></sup> Also, the majority of the world's aircraft are not large jetliners but smaller piston aircraft, and with major modifications many are capable of using <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> as a fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SDSUProjects_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-SDSUProjects-62">[62]</a></sup> Another consideration is the vast amount of land that would be necessary to provide the biomass feedstock needed to support the needs of aviation, both civil and military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2008, an <a href="/wiki/Air_New_Zealand" title="Air New Zealand">Air New Zealand</a> jet completed the world's first commercial aviation test flight partially using <a href="/wiki/Jatropha" title="Jatropha">jatropha</a>-based fuel. Jatropha, used for biodiesel, can thrive on marginal agricultural land where many trees and crops won't grow, or would produce only slow growth yields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jat_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-jat-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ark_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ark-65">[65]</a></sup> Air New Zealand set several general sustainability criteria for its Jatropha, saying that such biofuels must not compete with food resources, that they must be as good as traditional jet fuels, and that they should be cost competitive with existing fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 2009, <a href="/wiki/Continental_Airlines" title="Continental Airlines">Continental Airlines</a> used a sustainable biofuel to power a commercial aircraft for the first time in North America. This marks the first sustainable biofuel demonstration flight by a commercial carrier using a twin-engined aircraft, a <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Next_Generation" title="Boeing 737 Next Generation">Boeing 737-800</a>, powered by CFM International CFM56-7B engines. The biofuel blend included components derived from algae and jatropha plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>One fuel biofuel alternative to <a href="/wiki/Avgas" title="Avgas">avgas</a> that is under development is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swift_Fuel" title="Swift Fuel">Swift Fuel</a>. Swift fuel was approved as a test fuel by <a href="/wiki/ASTM_International" title="ASTM International">ASTM International</a> in December 2009, allowing the company to continue their research and to pursue certification testing. Mary Rusek, president and co-owner of Swift Enterprises predicted at that time that "100SF will be comparably priced, environmentally friendlier and more fuel-efficient than other general aviation fuels on the market".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AvWeb15Dec09_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-AvWeb15Dec09-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InsideIndiana15Dec09_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-InsideIndiana15Dec09-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of June 2011, revised international aviation fuel standards officially allow commercial airlines to blend conventional jet fuel with up to 50 percent biofuels. The renewable fuels "can be blended with conventional commercial and military jet fuel through requirements in the newly issued edition of ASTM D7566, Specification for Aviation Turbine Fuel Containing Synthesized Hydrocarbons".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2011, the FAA announced it is awarding $7.7 million to eight companies to advance the development of drop-in commercial aviation biofuels, with a special focus on ATJ (alcohol to jet) fuel. As part of its CAAFI (Commercial Aviation Alternative Fuel Initiative) and CLEEN (Continuous Lower Emissions, Energy and Noise) programs, the FAA plans to assist in the development of a sustainable fuel (from alcohols, sugars, biomass, and organic matter such as pyrolysis oils) that can be "dropped in" to aircraft without changing current infrastructure. The grant will also be used to research how the fuels affect engine durability and quality control standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Finally, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquified_natural_gas" title="Liquified natural gas">liquified natural gas</a> is another fuel that is used in some airplanes. Besides the lower GHG emissions (depending from where the natural gas was obtained from), another major benefit to airplane operators is the price, which is far lower than the price for jet fuel.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reducing_air_travel">Reducing air travel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Reducing air travel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_choices_and_social_pressure">Personal choices and social pressure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Personal choices and social pressure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The German video short <i>The Bill</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> explores how travel and its impacts are commonly viewed in everyday developed-world life, and the social pressures that are at play. British writer George Marshall has investigated common rationalizations that act as barriers to making personal choices to travel less, or to justify recent trips. In an informal research project, "one you are welcome to join", he says, he deliberately steered conversations with people who are attuned to climate change problems to questions about recent long-distance flights and why the travel was justified. Reflecting on actions contrary to their beliefs, he noted, "(i)ntriguing as their dissonance may be, what is especially revealing is that every one of these people has a career that is predicated on the assumption that information is sufficient to generate change – an assumption that a moment's introspection would show them was deeply flawed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_and_professional_choices">Business and professional choices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Business and professional choices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>With most international conferences having hundreds if not thousands of participants, and the bulk of these usually traveling by plane, conference travel is an area where significant reductions in air-travel-related GHG emissions could be made....This does not mean non-attendance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reay-2004_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reay-2004-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>For example, by 2003 <a href="/wiki/Access_Grid" title="Access Grid">Access Grid technology</a> has already been successfully used to host several international conferences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reay-2004_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reay-2004-74">[74]</a></sup> and technology has likely progressed substantially since then. The Tyndall Centre for Climate Change Research has been systematically studying means to change common institutional and professional practices that have led to large carbon footprints of travel by research scientists, and issued a report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Ending_incentives_to_fly.E2.80.94frequent_flyer_programs"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ending_incentives_to_fly—frequent_flyer_programs">Ending incentives to fly—frequent flyer programs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Ending incentives to fly—frequent flyer programs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Over 130 airlines have "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frequent_flyer_program" title="Frequent flyer program">frequent flyer programs</a>" based at least in part on miles, kilometers, points or segments for flights taken. Globally, such programs included about 163 million people as reported in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-economist-78">[78]</a></sup>  These programs benefit airlines by habituating people to air travel and, through the mechanics of partnerships with credit card companies and other businesses, in which high profit margin revenue streams can amount to selling free seats for a high price.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-economist-78">[78]</a></sup> The only part of <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> business that was making money when the company filed for bankruptcy in 2002 was its frequent flyer program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-economist-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Concerning business travel, "The ease of international air travel and the fact that, for most of us, the costs are met by our employers, means that ... globe trotting conference travel is often regarded as a perk of the job."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reay-2004_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Reay-2004-74">[74]</a></sup> However, the perk usually is not only the business trip itself, but also the frequent flyer points which the individual accrues by taking the trip, and which can be redeemed later for personal air travel. Thus a <a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_interest" title="Conflict of interest">conflict of interest</a> is established, whereby bottom-up pressure may be created within a firm or government agency for travel that is really not necessary. Even when such conflict is not a motivation, the perk of frequent flyer miles can be expected to lead in many cases to personal trips that would not be taken if a ticket had to be paid for with personal funds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>By just using an airline-sponsored credit card to pay one's household expenses, personal or business bills, or even expense bills charged to an employer, frequent flyer points can be racked up quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-economist-78">[78]</a></sup> Thus, free travel—for which the individual has to pay nothing extra—becomes a reality. Across society, this too can be expected to lead to much air travel—and greenhouse gas emissions—that otherwise would not occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several studies have contemplated the elimination of frequent flyer programmes (FFPs), on the grounds of anti-competitiveness,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Storm_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Storm-81">[81]</a></sup> ethics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cognac_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cognac-82">[82]</a></sup> conflict with society's overall well-being,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Treth_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Treth-83">[83]</a></sup> or climate effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-84">[84]</a></sup> There is a record of governments disallowing or banning FFPs and of industry players requesting bans. Denmark did not allow the programs until 1992, then changing its policy because its airlines were disadvantaged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Storm_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Storm-81">[81]</a></sup> In 2002, Norway banned domestic FFPs in order to promote competition among its airlines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> In the U.S. in 1989, a vice president of Braniff "said the government should consider ordering an end to frequent-flyer programs, which he said allow unfair competition."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>A Canadian study said that because of competition no airline could unilaterally end its FFP, but that a national government could use its regulatory power to end the programs broadly, which in Canada's case would also require North America-wide cooperation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Treth_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Treth-83">[83]</a></sup> In further analysis, a Scandinavian study which recommended an end to frequent flyer plans said, "the only possible way of prohibiting FFPs successfully now that they have spread from the US to Europe to the Far East would be to do so on a global basis. The basis exists: it could be done by the World Trade Organization."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Storm_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-Storm-81">[81]</a></sup> A recent study which surveyed frequent flyers in the U.K. and Norway, looked into behavioral addition to frequent flying and the "flyer's dilemma" of the conflict between "the social and personal benefits of flying and air travel's impact on climate change."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-84">[84]</a></sup> It concluded that:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Continued growth in both frequent flying practices and concern over air travel's climate impacts are in a dynamic relationship and the question of whether one or the other will reach a tipping point cannot yet be determined. Self-regulation, external regulation, social norms, technology and physical resources will continue to co-constitute the balance. An increasing stigmatisation of 'excessive' air travel may (re)frame flying as more open to collective external mitigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>This means government action.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_for_governmental_constraints_on_demand">Potential for governmental constraints on demand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Potential for governmental constraints on demand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>One means for reducing the environmental impact of aviation is to constrain demand for air travel, through increased fares in place of expanded airport capacity. Several studies have explored this:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">UK</a> study <i>Predict and Decide – Aviation, climate change and UK policy</i>, notes that a 10% increase in fares generates a 5% to 15% reduction in demand, and recommends that the British government should manage demand rather than provide for it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> This would be accomplished via a strategy that presumes "… against the expansion of UK airport capacity" and constrains demand by the use of economic instruments to price air travel less attractively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></li>
<li>A study published by the campaign group <a href="/wiki/Aviation_Environment_Federation" title="Aviation Environment Federation">Aviation Environment Federation</a> (AEF) concludes that by levying £9 billion of additional taxes, the annual rate of growth in demand in the UK for air travel would be reduced to 2%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></li>
<li>The ninth report of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Audit_Select_Committee" title="Environmental Audit Select Committee">Environmental Audit Select Committee</a>, published in July 2006, recommends that the British government rethink its airport expansion policy and considers ways, particularly via increased taxation, in which future demand can be managed in line with industry performance in achieving fuel efficiencies, so that emissions are not allowed to increase in absolute terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_regulation_of_air_travel_GHG_emissions">International regulation of air travel GHG emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: International regulation of air travel GHG emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kyoto_Protocol_2005">Kyoto Protocol 2005</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Kyoto Protocol 2005">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Greenhouse gas emissions from fuel consumption in international aviation, in contrast to those from domestic aviation and from energy use by airports, are excluded from the scope of the first period (2008–2012) of the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a>, as are the non-CO<sub>2</sub> climate effects. Instead, governments agreed to work through the <a href="/wiki/International_Civil_Aviation_Organization" title="International Civil Aviation Organization">International Civil Aviation Organization</a> (ICAO) to limit or reduce emissions and to find a solution to the allocation of emissions from international aviation in time for the second period of the Kyoto Protocol starting from 2009; however, the Copenhagen climate conference failed to reach an agreement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent research points to this failure as a substantial obstacle to global policy including a CO<sub>2</sub> emissions reduction pathway that would <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avoiding_dangerous_climate_change" title="Avoiding dangerous climate change">avoid dangerous climate change</a> by keeping the increase in the average global temperature below a 2 °C rise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Approaches_toward_emissions_trading">Approaches toward emissions trading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Approaches toward emissions trading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As part of that process the ICAO has endorsed the adoption of an open <a href="/wiki/Emissions_trading" title="Emissions trading">emissions trading</a> system to meet CO<sub>2</sub> emissions reduction objectives. Guidelines for the adoption and implementation of a global scheme are currently being developed, and will be presented to the ICAO Assembly in 2007,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> although the prospects of a comprehensive inter-governmental agreement on the adoption of such a scheme are uncertain.
</p><p>Within the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, however, the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> has resolved to incorporate aviation in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Union_Emissions_Trading_Scheme" title="European Union Emissions Trading Scheme">European Union Emissions Trading Scheme</a> (ETS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> A new directive was adopted by the European Parliament in July 2008 and approved by the Council in October 2008. It became effective on 1 January 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Researchers at the <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Development_Institute" title="Overseas Development Institute">Overseas Development Institute</a> investigated the possible effects on <a href="/wiki/Small_Island_Developing_States" title="Small Island Developing States">Small Island Developing States</a> (SIDS) of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>'s decision to limit the supply of <a href="/wiki/Certified_Emission_Reduction" title="Certified Emission Reduction">Certified Emission Reductions</a> (CERs) to its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Union_Emissions_Trading_Scheme" title="European Union Emissions Trading Scheme">ETS</a>  market to <a href="/wiki/Least_Developed_Countries" title="Least Developed Countries">Least Developed Countries</a> (LDCs) from 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Most SIDS are highly <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability" title="Vulnerability">vulnerable</a> to the effects of <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> and rely heavily on <a href="/wiki/Tourism" title="Tourism">tourism</a> as a basis for their economies, so this decision could place them at some disadvantage. The researchers therefore highlight the need to ensure that any regulatory frameworks put in place to tackle climate change take into account the development needs of the most vulnerable countries affected.
</p><p>A report published by researchers at the <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_Aviation,_Transport_and_Environment" title="Centre for Aviation, Transport and Environment">Centre for Aviation, Transport and Environment</a> at <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Metropolitan_University" title="Manchester Metropolitan University">Manchester Metropolitan University</a> found that the only way to have a significant impact on emissions was to put a price on carbon and to use a market-based measure (MBM), such as the EU Emissions Trading Scheme (ETS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="International_Civil_Aviation_Organization_agreement_2016">International Civil Aviation Organization agreement 2016</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: International Civil Aviation Organization agreement 2016">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In October 2016 the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a> agency <a href="/wiki/International_Civil_Aviation_Organization" title="International Civil Aviation Organization">International Civil Aviation Organization</a> (ICAO) finalized an agreement among its 191 member nations to address the more than <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7011458000000000000♠</span>458 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">Mt</a></span> (2010)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> of carbon dioxide emitted annually by international passenger and cargo flights. The agreement will use an offsetting scheme called CORSIA (the Carbon Offsetting and Reduction Scheme for International Aviation) under which forestry and other carbon-reducing activities are directly funded, amounting to about 2% of annual revenues for the sector. Rules against 'double counting' should ensure that existing forest protection efforts are not recycled. The scheme does not take effect until 2021 and will be voluntary until 2027, but many countries, including the US and China, have promised to begin at its 2020 inception date.   Under the agreement, the global aviation emissions target is a 50% reduction by 2050 relative to 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">NGO</a> reaction to the deal was mixed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-milman-2016_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-milman-2016-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The agreement has critics. It is not aligned with the 2015 Paris climate agreement, which set the objective of restricting global warming to 1.5 to 2 °C. A late draft of the agreement would have required the air transport industry to assess its share of global carbon budgeting to meet that objective, but the text was removed in the agreed version.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ccn_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-ccn-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conversation_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-conversation-104">[104]</a></sup> CORSIA will regulate only about 25 percent of aviation's international emissions, since it grandfather's all emissions below the 2020 level, allowing unregulated growth until then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Only 65 nations will participate in the initial voluntary period, not including significant emitters Russia, India and perhaps Brazil. The agreement does not cover domestic emissions, which are 40% of the global industry's overall emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conversation_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-conversation-104">[104]</a></sup> One observer of the ICAO convention made this summary, "Airline claims that flying will now be green are a myth. Taking a plane is the fastest and cheapest way to fry the planet and this deal won't reduce demand for jet fuel one drop. Instead offsetting aims to cut emissions in other industries,..." Another critic called it "a timid step in the right direction."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_climate_change_on_aviation">Effects of climate change on aviation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Effects of climate change on aviation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Increased_turbulence">Increased turbulence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Increased turbulence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A report published in the science journal <i>Nature Climate Change</i> forecasts that increasing CO<sub>2</sub> levels will result in a significant increase in in-flight turbulence experienced by transatlantic airline flights by the middle of the 21st century. The lead author of the study, Paul Williams, a researcher at the National Center for Atmospheric Science, at the University of Reading stated, "air turbulence does more than just interrupt the service of in-flight drinks. It injures hundreds of passengers and aircrew every year – sometimes fatally. It also causes delays and damage to planes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RT09Apr13_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-RT09Apr13-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Noise">Noise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Noise">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_noise" title="Aircraft noise">Aircraft noise</a></div>
<p>Aircraft noise is seen by advocacy groups as being very hard to get attention and action on. The fundamental issues are increased traffic at larger airports and airport expansion at smaller and regional airports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
Aviation authorities and airlines have developed Continuous Descent Approach procedures <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> to reduce noise footprint. Current applicable noise standards effective since 2014 are FAA Stage 4 and (equivalent) EASA Chapter 4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Aircraft with lower standards are restricted to a time window or, on many airports, banned completely. Stage 5 will become effective between 2017-2020. 
Quantification and comparison of noise effects per seat-distance takes takes into account that noise from cruise levels usually does not reach the earth surface (as opposed to surface-transportation) but is concentrated on and in proximity of airports.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_pollution">Water pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Water pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
