<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_U.S._Army_C-37B_aircraft_transporting_Army_Chief_of_Staff_Gen._Raymond_T._Odierno,_gets_de-iced_before_it_departs_Joint_Base_Elmendorf-Richardson,_Alaska.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1542" data-file-width="2313" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/A_U.S._Army_C-37B_aircraft_transporting_Army_Chief_of_Staff_Gen._Raymond_T._Odierno%2C_gets_de-iced_before_it_departs_Joint_Base_Elmendorf-Richardson%2C_Alaska.jpg/220px-A_U.S._Army_C-37B_aircraft_transporting_Army_Chief_of_Staff_Gen._Raymond_T._Odierno%2C_gets_de-iced_before_it_departs_Joint_Base_Elmendorf-Richardson%2C_Alaska.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/A_U.S._Army_C-37B_aircraft_transporting_Army_Chief_of_Staff_Gen._Raymond_T._Odierno%2C_gets_de-iced_before_it_departs_Joint_Base_Elmendorf-Richardson%2C_Alaska.jpg/330px-A_U.S._Army_C-37B_aircraft_transporting_Army_Chief_of_Staff_Gen._Raymond_T._Odierno%2C_gets_de-iced_before_it_departs_Joint_Base_Elmendorf-Richardson%2C_Alaska.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/A_U.S._Army_C-37B_aircraft_transporting_Army_Chief_of_Staff_Gen._Raymond_T._Odierno%2C_gets_de-iced_before_it_departs_Joint_Base_Elmendorf-Richardson%2C_Alaska.jpg/440px-A_U.S._Army_C-37B_aircraft_transporting_Army_Chief_of_Staff_Gen._Raymond_T._Odierno%2C_gets_de-iced_before_it_departs_Joint_Base_Elmendorf-Richardson%2C_Alaska.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_U.S._Army_C-37B_aircraft_transporting_Army_Chief_of_Staff_Gen._Raymond_T._Odierno,_gets_de-iced_before_it_departs_Joint_Base_Elmendorf-Richardson,_Alaska.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aircraft deicing. Excess deicing fluid may contaminate nearby water bodies, if not properly recovered.</div></div></div>
<p>Airports can generate significant <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a> due to their extensive use and handling of jet fuel, lubricants and other chemicals. Airports install spill control structures and related equipment (e.g., vacuum trucks, portable berms, absorbents) to prevent chemical spills, and mitigate the impacts of spills that do occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA-stormwater-fact_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-stormwater-fact-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>In cold climates, the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De-ice" title="De-ice">deicing fluids</a> can also cause water pollution, as most of the fluids applied to aircraft subsequently fall to the ground and can be carried via <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">stormwater runoff</a> to nearby streams, rivers or coastal waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_airport-TDD_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_airport-TDD-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>101</span></sup> Airlines use deicing fluids based on <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_glycol" title="Ethylene glycol">ethylene glycol</a> or <a href="/wiki/Propylene_glycol" title="Propylene glycol">propylene glycol</a> as the active ingredient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_airport-TDD_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_airport-TDD-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>4</span></sup>
</p><p>Ethylene glycol and propylene glycol are known to exert high levels of <a href="/wiki/Biochemical_oxygen_demand" title="Biochemical oxygen demand">biochemical oxygen demand</a> (BOD) during degradation in surface waters. This process can adversely affect aquatic life by consuming oxygen needed by aquatic organisms for survival. Large quantities of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissolved_oxygen" title="Dissolved oxygen">dissolved oxygen</a> (DO) in the <a href="/wiki/Water_column" title="Water column">water column</a> are consumed when microbial populations decompose propylene glycol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_airport-environmental_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_airport-environmental-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>2–23</span></sup>
</p><p>Sufficient dissolved oxygen levels in surface waters are critical for the survival of fish, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macroinvertebrate" title="Macroinvertebrate">macroinvertebrates</a>, and other aquatic organisms. If oxygen concentrations drop below a minimum level, organisms emigrate, if able and possible, to areas with higher oxygen levels or eventually die. This effect can drastically reduce the amount of usable aquatic habitat. Reductions in DO levels can reduce or eliminate <a href="/wiki/Bottom_feeder" title="Bottom feeder">bottom feeder</a> populations, create conditions that favor a change in a community’s species profile, or alter critical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food-web" title="Food-web">food-web</a> interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_airport-environmental_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_airport-environmental-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>2–30</span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_quality">Air quality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Air quality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">Air pollution</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Particulate_emissions">Particulate emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Particulate emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultrafine_particles" title="Ultrafine particles">Ultrafine particles</a> (UFPs) are emitted by aircraft engines during near-surface level operations including <a href="/wiki/Taxiing" title="Taxiing">taxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Takeoff" title="Takeoff">takeoff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climb_(aeronautics)" title="Climb (aeronautics)">climb</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descent_(aircraft)" title="Descent (aircraft)">descent</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Landing" title="Landing">landing</a>, as well as idling at gates and on taxiways. Other sources of UFPs include ground support equipment operating around the terminal areas. In 2014, an <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air quality</a> study found the area impacted by ultrafine particles from the takeoffs and landings downwind of <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_International_Airport" title="Los Angeles International Airport">Los Angeles International Airport</a> to be of much greater magnitude than previously thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Typical UFP emissions during takeoff are on the order of 10<sup>15</sup>-10<sup>17</sup> particles emitted per kilogram of fuel burned. Non-volatile soot particle emissions are 10<sup>14</sup>-10<sup>16</sup> particles per kilogram fuel on a number basis and 0.1-1 gram per kilogram fuel on a mass basis, depending on the engine and fuel characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lead_emissions">Lead emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Lead emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Avgas#Environmental_regulation" title="Avgas">Avgas § Environmental regulation</a></div>
<p>Some 167,000 piston engine aircraft—about three-quarters of private planes in the United States—release lead (Pb) into the air due to leaded aviation fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>  From 1970 to 2007, general aviation aircraft emitted about 34,000 tons of lead into the atmosphere according to the Environmental Protection Agency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Lead is recognized as a serious environmental threat by the Federal Aviation Administration if inhaled or ingested leading to adverse effects on the nervous system, red blood cells and cardiovascular and immune systems with infants and young children especially sensitive to even low levels of lead, which may contribute to behavioral and learning problems, lower IQ<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> and autism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation_exposure">Radiation exposure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Radiation exposure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Flying 12 kilometres (39,000 ft) high, passengers and crews of <a href="/wiki/Jet_airliner" title="Jet airliner">jet airliners</a> are exposed to at least 10 times the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic ray</a> dose that people at <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a> receive. Every few years, a <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_storm" title="Geomagnetic storm">geomagnetic storm</a> permits a <a href="/wiki/Solar_particle_event" title="Solar particle event">solar particle event</a> to penetrate down to jetliner altitudes. Aircraft flying <a href="/wiki/Polar_route" title="Polar route">polar routes</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_pole" title="Geomagnetic pole">geomagnetic poles</a> are at particular risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_use_for_infrastructure">Land use for infrastructure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Land use for infrastructure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Airport buildings, taxiways and runways take possession of a part of our ecosystem. Most of aircraft movement however is positioned in air at altitude and so away from direct interaction with sensitive nature or human detection. This opposed to roads, railways and canals being very significant in use of area and dividing of ecological structures while required for surface transportation for as many miles as the distance traveled.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmental_impact_of_aviation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="metadata mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">

<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="30" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/30px-Office-book.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/45px-Office-book.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/60px-Office-book.svg.png 2x" width="30"/></td>

<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://ec.europa.eu/research/growth/pdf/acare_press_release_revised_8-11.pdf" rel="nofollow">Advisory Council for Aeronautical Research in Europe (ACARE) <i>Strategic Research Agenda</i> (2002)</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20071128074432/http://ec.europa.eu/research/growth/pdf/acare_press_release_revised_8-11.pdf" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 28 November 2007 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>. "These objectives include, inter alia, a 50% cut in CO<sub>2</sub> and 80% in Nox emissions" [for new aircraft introduced in 2020 relative to new aircraft introduced in 2000].</span>

<dl><dt>Concerns</dt></dl>
